<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Pytheas</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox biography vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Pytheas of Massalia</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg"><img alt="Pythéas.jpg" data-file-height="3146" data-file-width="1964" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg/200px-Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg/300px-Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg/400px-Pyth%C3%A9as.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a><div>Statue of Pytheas outside the <a href="/wiki/Palais_de_la_Bourse_(Marseille)" title="Palais de la Bourse (Marseille)">Palais de la Bourse</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marseilles" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Born</th><td>Approximately 350 BC<br/><div class="birthplace" style="display:inline"><a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massalia</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Died</th><td>Approximately 285 BC</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Nationality</th><td class="category"><a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Citizenship</th><td class="category">Massaliote</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Known for</th><td>Earliest <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> voyage to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> for which there is a record, author of <i>Periplus</i>.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><b>Scientific career</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Fields</th><td class="category">Geography, exploration, navigation</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Influenced</th><td>Subsequent classical geographers and explorers</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Pytheas of Massalia</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/θ/: 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>: Πυθέας ὁ Μασσαλιώτης <i>Pythéas ho Massaliōtēs</i>; <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>: <i>Pytheas Massiliensis</i>; fl. 4th century BC) was a <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Graeco-Roman_geographers" title="List of Graeco-Roman geographers">geographer</a> and explorer from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" title="Greek colonies">Greek colony</a> of Massalia (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>). He made a voyage of exploration to northwestern Europe in about 325 BC, but his account of it, known widely in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Antiquity</a>, has not survived and is now known only through the writings of others.
</p><p>On this voyage, he circumnavigated and visited a considerable part of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. He was the first known scientific visitor to see and describe the Arctic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_ice" title="Polar ice">polar ice</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes. He is also the first person on record to describe the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midnight_Sun" title="Midnight Sun">Midnight Sun</a>. The theoretical existence of some Northern phenomena that he described, such as a frigid zone, and temperate zones where the nights are very short in summer and the sun does not set at the summer solstice, was already known. Similarly, reports of a country of perpetual snow and darkness (the country of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperboreans" title="Hyperboreans">Hyperboreans</a>) had reached the Mediterranean some centuries before. 
</p><p>Pytheas introduced the idea of distant <a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Thule</a> to the geographic imagination, and his account of the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> is the earliest one known that suggests the moon as their cause. He also may have reached Iceland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates">Dates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Dates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> says that <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(historian)" title="Timaeus (historian)">Timaeus</a> (born about 350 BC) believed Pytheas' story of the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pliny37-11_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-pliny37-11-2">[2]</a></sup> First century BC <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> says that <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">Dicaearchus</a> (died about 285 BC) did not trust the stories of Pytheas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> That is all the information that survives concerning the date of Pytheas' voyage. Presuming that Timaeus would not have written until after he was 20 years old in about 330 BC and Dicaearchus would have needed time to write his most mature work, after 300 BC, there is no reason not to accept <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fanshawe_Tozer" title="Henry Fanshawe Tozer">Henry Fanshawe Tozer</a>'s interval of 330–300 BC for the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tozerxxi_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tozerxxi-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (December 2017)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup> Some would give Timaeus an extra 5 years, bringing the voyage to 325 BC at earliest. There is no further evidence.
</p><p>If one presumes that Pytheas would not have written before reaching age 20, he would have been a contemporary and competitor of Timaeus and Dicaearchus. As they read his writings he must have written toward the earlier years of the interval.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Record">Record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Strabon_Rerum_geographicarum_1620.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="310" decoding="async" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Strabon_Rerum_geographicarum_1620.jpg/220px-Strabon_Rerum_geographicarum_1620.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Strabon_Rerum_geographicarum_1620.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Strabon_Rerum_geographicarum_1620.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1620 edition of <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographica</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Pytheas described his travels in a work that has not survived; only excerpts remain, quoted or paraphrased by later authors, most familiarly in <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographica</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Pliny's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalis_Historia" title="Naturalis Historia">Natural History</a></i> and passages in <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus of Sicily</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">history</a>. Most of the ancients, including the first two just mentioned, refer to his work by his name: "Pytheas says …" Two late writers give titles: the astronomical author <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geminus_of_Rhodes" title="Geminus of Rhodes">Geminus of Rhodes</a> mentions <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">τὰ περὶ τοῦ Ὠκεανοῦ</span> (<i>ta peri tou Okeanou</i>), literally "things about the Ocean", sometimes translated as "Description of the Ocean", "On the Ocean" or "Ocean"; <a href="/wiki/Marcian_of_Heraclea" title="Marcian of Heraclea">Marcianus</a>, the scholiast on <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, mentions <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">περίοδος γῆς</span> (<i>periodos gēs</i>), a "trip around the earth" or <a href="/wiki/Periplus" title="Periplus">περίπλους (<i>periplous</i>), "sail around"</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Scholars of the 19th century tended to interpret these titles as the names of distinct works covering separate voyages; for example, Smith's <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Greek_and_Roman_Biography_and_Mythology" title="Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology">Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology</a></i> hypothesizes a voyage to Britain and Thule written about in "Ocean" and another from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiz" title="Cadiz">Cadiz</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">Don River</a>, written about in "Sail Around".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithpyth_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithpyth-6">[6]</a></sup> As is common with ancient texts, multiple titles may represent a single source, for example, if a title refers to a section rather than the whole. Presently <a href="/wiki/Periplus" title="Periplus">periplus</a> is recognized as a genre of navigational literature. Mainstream consensus is that there was only one work, "on the Ocean", which was based on a periplus.
</p><p>Diodorus does not mention Pytheas by name. The association is made as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Pliny reports that "Timaeus says there is an island named Mictis … where tin is found, and to which the Britons cross."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plinyIV-30_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-plinyIV-30-8">[8]</a></sup> Diodorus says that tin is brought to the island of <a href="/wiki/Ictis" title="Ictis">Ictis</a>, where there is an emporium. The last link is supplied by Strabo, who says that an emporium on the island of Corbulo in the mouth of the river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loire_river" title="Loire river">Loire</a> was associated with the Britain of Pytheas by <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Assuming that Ictis, Mictis and Corbulo are the same, Diodorus appears to have read Timaeus, who must have read Pytheas, whom Polybius also read.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Circumstances_of_the_voyage">Circumstances of the voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Circumstances of the voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_pre-Roman_Gaul" title="Greeks in pre-Roman Gaul">Greeks in pre-Roman Gaul</a></div>
<p>Pytheas was the first documented Mediterranean mariner to reach the British Isles.
</p><p>The start of Pytheas's voyage is unknown. The <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginians</a> had closed the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> to all ships from other nations. Some historians, mainly of the late 19th century and before, therefore speculated (without any evidence) that he must have traveled overland to the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Loire" title="Loire">Loire</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Garonne" title="Garonne">Garonne</a>. Others believed that, to avoid the Carthaginian blockade, he may have stayed close to land and sailed only at night, or taken advantage of a temporary lapse in the blockade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternate theory is that by the 4th century BC, the western Greeks, especially the Massaliotes, were on amicable terms with Carthage. In 348 BC, Carthage and Rome came to terms over the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Wars" title="Sicilian Wars">Sicilian Wars</a> with a treaty defining their mutual interests. Rome could use Sicilian markets, Carthage could buy and sell goods at Rome, and slaves taken by Carthage from allies of Rome were to be set free. Rome was to stay out of the western Mediterranean, but these terms did not apply to Massalia, which had its own treaty. During the second half of the 4th century BC, the time of Pytheas' voyage, Massaliotes were presumably free to operate as they pleased; there is, at least, no evidence of conflict with Carthage in any of the sources that mention the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early part of Pytheas' voyage is outlined by statements of <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> that Strabo says are false because taken from<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Pytheas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Apparently, Pytheas said that tides ended at the "sacred promontory" (<i>Hieron akrōtērion</i>, or <a href="/wiki/Sagres_Point" title="Sagres Point">Sagres Point</a>), and from there to <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Gades</a> is said to be 5 days' sail. Strabo complains about this distance, and about Pytheas' portrayal of the exact location of <a href="/wiki/Tartessos" title="Tartessos">Tartessos</a>. Mention of these places in a journal of the voyage indicates that Pytheas passed through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straits_of_Gibraltar" title="Straits of Gibraltar">Straits of Gibraltar</a> and sailed north along the coast of <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_Britain">Discovery of Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Discovery of Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="The_.22circumnavigation.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='The_"circumnavigation"'>The "circumnavigation"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title='Edit section: The "circumnavigation"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="2283" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg/350px-Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg/525px-Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg/700px-Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy-british-isles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1490 Italian reconstruction of the map of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>. The map is a result of a combination of the lines of roads and of the coasting expeditions during the first century of Roman occupation. One great fault, however, is a lopsided Scotland, which in one hypothesis is the result of Ptolemy using Pytheas' measurements of latitude (<a href="/wiki/Pytheas#Pytheas'_measurements_of_latitude" title="Pytheas">see below.</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Whether Ptolemy would have had Pytheas' real latitudes at that time is a much debated issue.</div></div></div>
<p>Strabo reports that Pytheas says he "travelled over the whole of Britain that was accessible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-4-1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-4-1-14">[14]</a></sup> The word <i>epelthein</i>, at root "come upon", does not imply any specific method, and Pytheas does not elaborate. He does use the word "whole" and he states a <i>perimetros</i> ("perimeter") of more than 40,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stadia_(length)" title="Stadia (length)">stadia</a>. Using Herodotus' standard of 600 feet (180 m) for one stadium gives 4,545 miles (7,314 km); however, there is no way to tell which standard foot was in effect. The English foot is an approximation. Strabo wants to discredit Pytheas on the grounds that 40,000 stadia is outrageously high and cannot be real.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> gives a similar number:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> 42,500 stadia, about 4,830 miles (7,770 km), and explains that it is the perimeter of a triangle around Britain. The consensus has been that he probably took his information from Pytheas through <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(historian)" title="Timaeus (historian)">Timaeaus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> gives the <i>circuitus</i> reported by Pytheas as 4,875 <a href="/wiki/Mile#Roman_mile" title="Mile">Roman miles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The explorer <a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen" title="Fridtjof Nansen">Fridtjof Nansen</a> explains this apparent fantasy of Pytheas as a mistake of Timaeus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nansen51_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nansen51-17">[17]</a></sup> Strabo and Diodorus Siculus never saw Pytheas' work, says Nansen, but they and others read of him in Timaeus. Pytheas reported only days' sail. Timaeus converted days to stadia at the rate of 1,000 per day, a standard figure of the times. However, Pytheas only sailed 560 stadia per day for a total of 23,800, which in Nansen's view is consistent with 700 stadia per degree. Nansen later states that Pytheas must have stopped to obtain astronomical data; presumably, the extra time was spent ashore. Using the stadia of Diodorus Siculus, one obtains 42.5 days for the time that would be spent in circumnavigating Britain. (It may have been a virtual circumnavigation<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>; see under Thule below.)
</p><p>The perimeter, according to Nansen based on the 23,800 stadia, was 2,375 miles (3,822 km). This number is in the neighborhood of what a triangular perimeter ought to be, but it cannot be verified against anything Pytheas may have said, nor is Diodorus Siculus very precise about the locations of the legs. The "perimeter" is often translated as "coastline", but this translation is misleading. The coastline, following all the bays and inlets, is 7,723 miles (12,429 km) (see <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_the_United_Kingdom#Coastline" title="Geography of the United Kingdom">Geography of the United Kingdom</a>). Pytheas could have travelled any perimeter between that number and Diodorus'. <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> adds that Pytheas said he traversed the whole of Britain on foot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> of which he, Polybius, is skeptical. Despite Strabo's conviction of a lie, the perimeter said to have been given by Pytheas is not evidence of it. The issue of what he did say can never be settled until more fragments of Pytheas literature are found.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_description_of_the_British">Name and description of the British</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Name and description of the British">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
