</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template talk:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The first known written use of the word Britain is one of its uses was an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliteration</a> of the original P-Celtic term. It is believed to have appeared within a <a href="/wiki/Periplus" title="Periplus">periplus</a> by the geographer and explorer Pytheas of <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massalia</a>, but no copies of this work survive. The earliest existing records of the word are quotations of the periplus by later authors, such as those within Strabo's <i><a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographica</a></i>, Pliny's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalis_Historia" title="Naturalis Historia">Natural History</a></i> and Diodorus of Sicily's <i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">Bibliotheca historica</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> According to Strabo, Pytheas referred to Britain as <i>Bretannikē</i>, which is treated a feminine noun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJBrettanike_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJBrettanike-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo(1.4.2)_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo(1.4.2)-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo(4.2.1)_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo(4.2.1)-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo(4.4.1)_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo(4.4.1)-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pictish_Beast.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Pictish_Beast.svg/200px-Pictish_Beast.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Pictish_Beast.svg/300px-Pictish_Beast.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Pictish_Beast.svg/400px-Pictish_Beast.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pictish_Beast.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictish_beast" title="Pictish beast">Pictish beast</a> on an early medieval <a href="/wiki/Pictish_stone" title="Pictish stone">Pictish stone</a>.</div></div></div><p> "Britain" is most like <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i>Ynys Prydein</i>, "the island of Britain", in which is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-Celtic_hypothesis" title="P-Celtic hypothesis">P-Celtic</a> <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophone</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Q-Celtic_hypothesis" title="Q-Celtic hypothesis">Q-Celtic</a> Cruithne in Irish <i>Cruithen-tuath</i>, "land of the Picts". The base word is Scottish/Irish <i>cruth</i>, Welsh <i>pryd</i>, meaning "form". The British were the "people of forms", with the sense of shapes or pictures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> thought to refer to their practice of tattooing or war painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The Roman word <i>Picti</i>, "the Picts", means "painted".
</p><p>This etymology shows that Pytheas interacted not so much with Irish or Scots, as they used Q-Celtic. Rather, Pytheas brought back the P-Celtic form from more geographically accessible regions where Welsh or Breton are spoken presently.  Furthermore, some proto-Celtic was spoken over all of Greater Britain, and this particular spelling is prototypical of those more populous regions.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg/220px-St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg/330px-St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg/440px-St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St_Fagans_Celtic_Village_the_main_hut.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of a Celtic thatched hut in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Diodorus based on Pytheas reports that Britain is cold and subject to frosts, being "too much subject to the <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">Bear</a>", and not "under the Arctic pole", as some translations say.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diodV_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-diodV-26">[26]</a></sup>
The numerous population of natives, he says, live in thatched cottages, store their grain in subterranean caches and bake bread from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diodV_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-diodV-26">[26]</a></sup> They are "of simple manners" (<i>ēthesin haplous</i>) and are content with plain fare. They are ruled by many kings and princes who live in peace with each other. Their troops fight from chariots, as did the Greeks in the Trojan War.
</p>
<h3><span id="The_three_corners_of_Britain:_Kantion.2C_Belerion_and_Orkas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_three_corners_of_Britain:_Kantion,_Belerion_and_Orkas">The three corners of Britain: Kantion, Belerion and Orkas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The three corners of Britain: Kantion, Belerion and Orkas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Opposite Europe in Diodorus is the promontory (<i>akrōtērion</i>) of <i>Kantion</i> (<a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>), 100 stadia, about 11 miles (18 km), from the land, but the text is ambiguous: "the land" could be either Britain or the continent. Four days' sail beyond that is another promontory, <i>Belerion</i>, which can only be <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, as Diodorus is describing the triangular perimeter and the third point is <i>Orkas</i>, presumably the main island of the <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> Islands.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_tin_trade">The tin trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The tin trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The inhabitants of Cornwall are involved in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Tin_ingot" title="Tin ingot">tin ingots</a>. They mine the ore, smelt it and then work it into pieces the shape of knuckle-bones, after which it is transported to the island of <a href="/wiki/Ictis" title="Ictis">Ictis</a> by wagon, which can be done at low tide. Merchants purchasing it there pack it on horses for 30 days to the river <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a>, where it is carried down to the mouth. Diodorus says that the inhabitants of Cornwall are civilised in manner and especially hospitable to strangers because of their dealings with foreign merchants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_Thule">Discovery of Thule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Discovery of Thule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Strabo relates, taking his text from Polybius, "Pytheas asserts that he explored in person the whole northern region of Europe as far as the ends of the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-4-2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-4-2-27">[27]</a></sup> Strabo does not believe it but he explains what Pytheas means by the ends of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-5-8_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-5-8-28">[28]</a></sup> <i>Thoulē</i>, he says (now spelled <a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Thule</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> uses <i>Tyle</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vergil" title="Vergil">Vergil</a> references <i>ultima Thule</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgic" title="Georgic">Georgic</a> I, Line 30, where the <i>ultima</i> refer to the end of the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>) is the most northerly of the British Isles. There the circle of the summer tropic is the same as the Arctic Circle (see below on Arctic Circle). Moreover, says Strabo, none of the other authors mention Thule, a fact which he uses to discredit Pytheas, but which to moderns indicates Pytheas was the first explorer to arrive there and tell of it.
</p><p>Thule is described as an island six days' sailing north of Britain, near the frozen sea (<i>pepēguia thalatta</i>, "solidified sea").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboI-4-2_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-straboI-4-2-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> adds that it has no nights at midsummer when the sun is passing through the sign of the <a href="/wiki/Cancer_(constellation)" title="Cancer (constellation)">Crab</a> (at the summer solstice),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plinyIV-30_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-plinyIV-30-8">[8]</a></sup> a reaffirmation that it is on the Arctic Circle. He adds that the crossing to Thule starts at the island of <i>Berrice</i>, "the largest of all", which may be <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Lewis" title="Isle of Lewis">Lewis</a> in the outer <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a>. If <i>Berrice</i> was in the outer Hebrides, the crossing would have brought Pytheas to the coast of <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B8re_og_Romsdal" title="Møre og Romsdal">Møre og Romsdal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%B8ndelag" title="Trøndelag">Trøndelag</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, explaining how he managed to miss the <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a>. If this is his route, in all likelihood he did not actually circumnavigate Britain, but returned along the coast of Germany, accounting for his somewhat larger perimeter.
</p><p>Concerning the location of Thule, a discrepancy in data caused subsequent geographers some problems, and may be responsible for <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s distortion of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. Strabo reports that <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> places Thule at a parallel 11500 stadia (1305 miles, or 16.4°) north of the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Borysthenes" title="Borysthenes">Borysthenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboI-4-2_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-straboI-4-2-30">[30]</a></sup> The parallel running through that mouth also passes through Celtica and is Pytheas' base line. Using 3700 or 3800 stadia (approximately 420–430 miles or 5.3°–5.4°) north of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marseilles" title="Marseilles">Marseilles</a> for a base line obtains a latitude of 64.8° or 64.9° for Thule, well short of the Arctic Circle. It is in fact the latitude of Trondheim, where Pytheas may have made land.
</p><p>A statement by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geminus_of_Rhodes" title="Geminus of Rhodes">Geminus of Rhodes</a> quotes <i>On the Ocean</i> as saying:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>... the Barbarians showed us the place where the sun goes to rest. For it was the case that in these parts the nights were very short, in some places two, in others three hours long, so that the sun rose again a short time after it had set.
</p>
</blockquote><p>
Nansen claimed that according to this statement, Pytheas was there in person and that the 21- and 22-hour days must be the customary statement of latitude by length of longest day. He calculates the latitudes to be 64° 32′ and 65° 31′, partially confirming Hipparchus' statement of the latitude of Thule. And yet Strabo says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-5-8_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-5-8-28">[28]</a></sup> </p><blockquote>
<p>Pytheas of Massalia tells us that Thule ... is farthest north, and that there the circle of the summer tropic is the same as the Arctic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Circle.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Eratosthenes extends the latitudinal distance from Massalia to Celtica to 5000 stadia (7.1°), placing the base line in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>. The northernmost location cited in Britain at the Firth of Clyde is now northern Scotland. To get this country south of Britain to conform to Strabo's interpretation of Pytheas, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> has to rotate Scotland by 90°.
</p><p>The 5000 stadia must be discounted: it crosses the Borysthenes upriver near <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a> rather than at the mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> It does place Pytheas on the Arctic Circle, which in Norway is south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lofoten_islands" title="Lofoten islands">Lofoten islands</a>. It seems that Eratosthenes altered the base line to pass through the northern extreme of Celtica. Pytheas, as related by Hipparchus, probably cited the place in Celtica where he first made land. If he used the same practice in Norway, Thule is at least somewhere on the entire northwest coast of Norway from <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B8re_og_Romsdal" title="Møre og Romsdal">Møre og Romsdal</a> to the Lofoten Islands.
</p><p>The explorer, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a>, in his study of Thule states that it has had many definitions over the centuries. Many more authors have written about it than remembered Pytheas. The question of the location of Pytheas' Thule remains. The latitudes given by the ancient authors can be reconciled. The missing datum required to fix the location is longitude: "Manifestly we cannot rely upon the longitude."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pytheas crossed the waters northward from Berrice, in the north of the British Isles, but whether to starboard, larboard, or straight ahead is not known. From the time of the Roman Empire all the possibilities were suggested repeatedly by each generation of writers: <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>. A manuscript variant of a name in Pliny has abetted the Iceland theory: <i>Nerigon</i> instead of <i>Berrice</i>, which sounds like Norway. If one sails west from Norway one encounters Iceland. Burton himself espoused this theory.
</p><p>The standard texts have <i>Berrice</i> presently, as well as <i>Bergos</i> for <i>Vergos</i> in the same list of islands. The <i>Scandiae</i> islands are more of a problem, as they could be Scandinavia, but other islands had that name as well. Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a> says (<i>De Bello Gothico</i>, Chapter 15) that the earlier name of Scandinavia was Thule and that it was the home of the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>. The fact that Pytheas returned from the vicinity of the Baltic favors Procopius's opinion. The fact that Pytheas lived centuries before the colonization of Iceland and Greenland by European agriculturalists makes them less likely candidates, as he stated that Thule was populated and its soil was tilled.
</p><p>Concerning the people of Thule Strabo says of Pytheas, but grudgingly:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>... he might possibly seem to have made adequate use of the facts as regards the people who live close to the frozen zone, when he says that, ... the people live on millet and other herbs, and on fruits and roots; and where there are grain and honey, the people get their beverage, also, from them. As for the grain, he says, – since they have no pure sunshine – they pound it out in large storehouses, after first gathering in the ears thither; for the threshing floors become useless because of this lack of sunshine and because of the rains.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>What he seems to be describing is an agricultural country that uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barn_(building)" title="Barn (building)">barns</a> for threshing grain rather than the Mediterranean outside floor of sun-baked mud and manufactures a drink, possibly <a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">mead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Encounter_with_drift_ice">Encounter with drift ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Encounter with drift ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Islossning_(119329313).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Islossning_%28119329313%29.jpg/250px-Islossning_%28119329313%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Islossning_%28119329313%29.jpg/375px-Islossning_%28119329313%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Islossning_%28119329313%29.jpg/500px-Islossning_%28119329313%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Islossning_(119329313).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pancake_ice" title="Pancake ice">Pancake ice</a> in the Baltic in spring near the <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> coast.</div></div></div>
<p>After mentioning the crossing (<i>navigatio</i>) from <i>Berrice</i> to <i>Tyle</i>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> makes a brief statement that:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p><i>A Tyle unius diei navigatione mare concretum a nonnullis Cronium appellatur.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote><p>"One day's sail from Thule is the frozen ocean, called by some the Cronian Sea."</p></blockquote>
<p>The <i>mare concretum</i> appears to match Strabo's <i>pepēguia thalatta</i> and is probably the same as the <i>topoi</i> ("places") mentioned in Strabo's apparent description of spring drift ice, which would have stopped his voyage further north and was for him the ultimate limit of the world. Strabo says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-4-1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-4-1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Pytheas also speaks of the waters around Thule and of those places where land properly speaking no longer exists, nor sea nor air, but a mixture of these things, like a "marine lung", in which it is said that earth and water and all things are in suspension as if this something was a link between all these elements, on which one can neither walk nor sail.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The term used for "marine lung" (<i>pleumōn thalattios</i>) appears to refer to <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a> of the type the ancients called sea-lung. The latter are mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> in <i>On the Parts of Animals</i> as being free-floating and insensate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> They are not further identifiable from what Aristotle says but some <i>pulmones</i> appear in Pliny as a class of insensate sea animal;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> specifically the <i>halipleumon</i> ("salt-water lung").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> William Ogle, a major translator and annotator of Aristotle, attributes the name sea-lung to the lung-like expansion and contraction of the <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">Medusae</a>, a kind of <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a>, during locomotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The ice resembled floating circles in the water. The modern English term for this phenomenon is <a href="/wiki/Pancake_ice" title="Pancake ice">pancake ice</a>.
</p><p>The association of Pytheas' observations with <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift ice</a> has long been standard in navigational literature, including <a href="/wiki/Bowditch%27s_American_Practical_Navigator" title="Bowditch's American Practical Navigator">Nathaniel Bowditch's <i>American Practical Navigator</i></a>, which begins Chapter 33, <i>Ice Navigation</i>, with Pytheas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> At its edge, sea, <a href="/wiki/Slush" title="Slush">slush</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a> mix, surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_the_Baltic">Discovery of the Baltic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Discovery of the Baltic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amber_hg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1514" data-file-width="1812" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Amber_hg.jpg/220px-Amber_hg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Amber_hg.jpg/330px-Amber_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Amber_hg.jpg/440px-Amber_hg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amber_hg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">Amber</a></div></div></div>
<p>Strabo says that Pytheas gave an account of "what is beyond the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_river" title="Rhine river">Rhine</a> as far as Scythia", which he, Strabo, thinks is false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboI-4-3_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-straboI-4-3-43">[43]</a></sup> In the geographers of the late Roman Republic and early Roman Empire, such as <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, Scythia stretches eastward from the mouth of the river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vistula_river" title="Vistula river">Vistula</a>; thus Pytheas must have described the Germanic coast of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" title="Baltic sea">Baltic sea</a>; if the statement is true, there are no other possibilities. As to whether he explored it in person, he said that he explored the entire north in person (see under Thule above). As the periplus was a sort of ship's log, he probably did reach the Vistula.
</p><p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">The Natural History</a></i> by Pliny the Elder:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pliny37-11_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-pliny37-11-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>
Pytheas says that the Gutones, a people of Germany, inhabit the shores of an estuary of the Ocean called Mentonomon, their territory extending a distance of six thousand stadia; that, at one day's sail from this territory, is the Isle of Abalus, upon the shores of which, <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> is thrown up by the waves in spring, it being an excretion of the sea in a concrete form; as, also, that the inhabitants use this <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> by way of fuel, and sell it to their neighbours, the Teutones.</p></blockquote>
<p>The "Gutones" is a simplification of two manuscript variants, <i>Guttonibus</i> and <i>Guionibus</i>, which would be in the <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a> <i>Guttones</i> or <i>Guiones</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> in the main opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The second major manuscript variant is either <i>Mentonomon</i> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative case</a>) or <i>Metuonidis</i> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a>). A number of etymologies have been proposed but none very well accepted. Amber is not actually named. It is termed the <i>concreti maris purgamentum</i>, the "frozen sea's leavings" after the springtime melt. Diodorus uses <i>ēlektron</i>, the Greek word for <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>, the object that gave its name to electricity through its ability to acquire a charge. Pliny is presenting an archaic opinion, as in his time amber was a precious stone brought from the Baltic at great expense, but the Germans, he says, use it for firewood, according to Pytheas.
</p><p>"Mentonomon" is unambiguously stated to be an <i>aestuarium</i> or "estuary" of 6000 stadia, which using the Herodotean standard of 600 feet (180 m) per stadium is 681 miles (1,096 km). That number happens to be the distance from the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a> to the mouth of the Vistula, but no source says explicitly where the figure was taken. Competing views, however, usually have to reinterpret "estuary" to mean something other than an estuary, as the west of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> is the only body of estuarial water of sufficient length in the region.
</p><p>Earlier Pliny says that a large island of three days' sail from the Scythian coast called <a href="/wiki/Baltia" title="Baltia">Balcia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon of Lampsacus</a> is called Basilia by Pytheas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4.27.13_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-P4.27.13-45">[45]</a></sup> It is generally understood to be the same as Abalus. Based on the amber, the island could have been <a href="/wiki/Heligoland" title="Heligoland">Heligoland</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zealand_(Denmark)" title="Zealand (Denmark)">Zealand</a>, the shores of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_of_Gdansk" title="Bay of Gdansk">Bay of Gdansk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sambia_Peninsula" title="Sambia Peninsula">Sambia</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Curonian_Lagoon" title="Curonian Lagoon">Curonian Lagoon</a>, which were historically the richest sources of amber in northern Europe. This is the earliest use of <i>Germania</i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voyage_to_the_Don">Voyage to the Don</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Voyage to the Don">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pytheas claimed to have explored the entire north; however, he turned back at the mouth of the Vistula, the border with <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>. If he had gone on he would have discovered the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Balts" title="Balts">Balts</a>. They occupied the lands to the east of the Vistula. In the west they began with the people living around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frisches_Haff" title="Frisches Haff">Frisches Haff</a>, Lithuanian Aismarės, "sea of the <a href="/wiki/Aesti" title="Aesti">Aistians</a>", who in that vicinity became the Baltic Prussians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> On the east <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> called them the Neuri, a name related to Old Prussian narus, "the deep", in the sense of water country. Later Lithuanians would be "the people of the shore". The Vistula was the traditional limit of Greater Germany. Place names featuring *<i>ner</i>- or *<i>nar</i>- are wide-ranging over the vast Proto-Baltic homeland, occupying western Russia before the Slavs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Herodotus says that the Neuri had Scythian customs, but they were at first not considered Scythian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> During the war between the Scythians and the Persian Empire, the Scythians came to dominate the Neuri. Strabo denies that any knowledge of the shores of the eastern Baltic existed. He had heard of the Sauromatai, but had no idea where to place them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Herodotus had mentioned these Sauromatai as a distinct people living near the Neuri. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, however, is much better informed. The island of Baunonia (<a href="/wiki/Bornholm" title="Bornholm">Bornholm</a>), he says lies a days' sail off Scythia, where amber is collected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P4.27.13_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-P4.27.13-45">[45]</a></sup> To him the limit of Germany is the Vistula. In contrast to Strabo, he knows that the Goths live around the Vistula, but these are definitely Germans.
</p><p>By the time of Tacitus, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aestii" title="Aestii">Aestii</a> have emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The former Scythia is now entirely Sarmatia. Evidently the Sarmatians have conquered westward to the Vistula. The Goths have moved to the south. That the Balts lived east of the Vistula from remote prehistoric times is unquestioned. The Baltic languages, however, are only known from the 2nd millennium AD. They are known to have developed in tribal contexts, as they were originally tribal. The first mention of any tribes is in Ptolemy's description of European Sarmatia, where the main Prussian tribes are mentioned for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In Tacitus, only the language of the Aestii is mentioned. Strabo distinguishes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venedi" title="Venedi">Venedi</a>, who were likely <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>. From these few references, which are the only surviving evidence apart from glottochronology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and place name analysis, it would seem that the Balts of Pytheas' time were well past the Common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic" title="Balto-Slavic">Balto-Slavic</a> stage, and likely spoke a number of related dialects. By turning back at what he thought was the limit of Germany, he not only missed the Balts, but did not discover that more Germans, the Goths, had moved into the Baltic area.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> relates: "... on his return thence (from the north), he traversed the whole of the coast of Europe from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiz" title="Cadiz">Gades</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">Tanais</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Some authors consider this leg a second voyage, as it does not seem likely he would pass by Marseilles without refitting and refreshing the crew. It is striking that he encountered the border of Scythia, turned around, and went around Europe counter-clockwise until he came to the southern side of Scythia on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. It is possible to speculate that he may have hoped to circumnavigate Europe, but the sources do not say. In other, even more speculative interpretations, Pytheas returned north and the Tanais is not the Don but is a northern river, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elbe_river" title="Elbe river">Elbe river</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithpyth_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-smithpyth-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurements_of_latitude">Measurements of latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Measurements of latitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitude_by_the_altitude_of_the_sun">Latitude by the altitude of the sun</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Latitude by the altitude of the sun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In discussing the work of Pytheas, Strabo typically uses direct discourse: "Pytheas says ..." In presenting his astronomical observations, he changes to indirect discourse: "<a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> says that Pytheas says ..." either because he never read Pytheas' manuscript (because it was not available to him) or in deference to Hipparchus, who appears to have been the first to apply the Babylonian system of representing the sphere of the earth by 360°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Strabo uses the degrees, based on Hipparchus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Neither say that Pytheas did. Nevertheless, Pytheas did obtain latitudes, which, according to Strabo, he expressed in proportions of the gnōmōn ("index"), or trigonometric <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions" title="Trigonometric functions">tangents</a> of angles of elevation to celestial bodies. They were measured on the gnōmōn, the vertical leg of a right triangle, and the flat leg of the triangle. The imaginary hypotenuse looked along the line of sight to the celestial body or marked the edge of a shadow cast by the vertical leg on the horizontal leg.
</p><p>Pytheas took the altitude of the sun at Massalia at noon on the longest day of the year and found that the tangent was the proportion of 120 (the length of the gnōmōn) to 1/5 less than 42 (the length of the shadow).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Hipparchus, relying on the authority of Pytheas (says Strabo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>), states that the ratio is the same as for <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> and that the two therefore are on the same parallel. Nansen and others prefer to give the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotangent" title="Cotangent">cotangent</a> 209/600,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nansen46_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-nansen46-58">[58]</a></sup> which is the inverse of the tangent, but the angle is greater than 45° and it is the tangent that Strabo states. His number system did not permit him to express it as a decimal but the tangent is about 2.87.
</p><p>It is unlikely that any of the geographers could compute the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctangent" title="Arctangent">arctangent</a>, or angle of that tangent. Moderns look it up in a table. Hipparchos is said to have had a table of some angles. The altitude, or angle of elevation, is 70° 47′ 50″<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nansen46_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-nansen46-58">[58]</a></sup> but that is not the <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>.
</p><p>At noon on the longest day the plane of longitude passing through Marseilles is exactly on edge to the sun. If the Earth's axis were not tilted toward the sun, a vertical rod at the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> would have no shadow. A rod further north would have a north-south shadow, and as an elevation of 90° would be a zero latitude, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_angles" title="Complementary angles">complement</a> of the elevation gives the latitude. The sun is even higher in the sky due to the tilt. The angle added to the elevation by the tilt is known as the <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt#Obliquity" title="Axial tilt">obliquity of the ecliptic</a> and at that time was 23° 44′ 40″.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nansen46_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-nansen46-58">[58]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complementary_angles" title="Complementary angles">complement</a> of the elevation less the obliquity is 43° 13′, only 5′ in error from Marseilles's latitude, 43° 18′.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitude_by_the_elevation_of_the_north_pole">Latitude by the elevation of the north pole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Latitude by the elevation of the north pole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A second method of determining the latitude of the observer measures the angle of elevation of a <a href="/wiki/Celestial_pole" title="Celestial pole">celestial pole</a>, north in the northern hemisphere. Seen from zero latitude the north pole's elevation is zero; that is, it is a point on the horizon. The <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> of the observer's <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> also is zero and therefore so is their latitude.
</p><p>As the observer's latitude increases (traveling north) so does the declination. The pole rises over the horizon by an angle of the same amount. The elevation at the terrestrial <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> is 90° (straight up) and the celestial pole has a declination of the same value. The latitude also is 90.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moderns have <a href="/wiki/Polaris" title="Polaris">Polaris</a> to mark the approximate location of the North celestial pole, which it does nearly exactly, but this position of Polaris was not available in Pytheas' time, due to changes in the positions of the stars. Pytheas reported that the pole was an empty space at the corner of a quadrangle, the other three sides of which were marked by stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Their identity has not survived but based on calculations these are believed to have been α and κ in <a href="/wiki/Draco_(constellation)" title="Draco (constellation)">Draco</a> and β in <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor" title="Ursa Minor">Ursa Minor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pytheas sailed northward with the intent of locating the Arctic Circle and exploring the "frigid zone" to the north of it at the extreme of the earth. He did not know the latitude of the circle in degrees. All he had to go by was the definition of the frigid zone as the latitudes north of the line where the celestial arctic circle was equal to the celestial Tropic of Cancer, the <i>tropikos kuklos</i> (refer to the next subsection). Strabo's angular report of this line as being at 24° may well be based on a tangent known to Pytheas, but he does not say that. In whatever mathematical form Pytheas knew the location, he could only have determined when he was there by taking periodic readings of the elevation of the pole (<i>eksarma tou polou</i> in Strabo and others).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Today the elevation can be obtained easily on ship with a <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(instrument)" title="Quadrant (instrument)">quadrant</a>. Electronic navigational systems have made even this simple measure unnecessary. Longitude was beyond Pytheas and his peers, but it was not of as great a consequence, because ships seldom strayed out of sight of land. East-west distance was a matter of contention to the geographers; they are one of Strabo's most frequent topics. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">gnōmōn</a> north-south distances were accurate often to within a degree.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>It is unlikely that any gnōmōn could be read accurately on the pitching deck of a small vessel at night. Pytheas must have made frequent overnight stops to use his gnōmōn and talk to the natives, which would have required interpreters, probably acquired along the way. The few fragments that have survived indicate that this material was a significant part of the <i>periplus</i>, possibly kept as the ship's log. There is little hint of native hostility; the Celts and the Germans appear to have helped him, which suggests that the expedition was put forward as purely scientific. In any case all voyages required stops for food, water and repairs; the treatment of voyagers fell under the special "guest" ethic for visitors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_of_the_Arctic_Circle">Location of the Arctic Circle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Location of the Arctic Circle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The ancient Greek view of the heavenly bodies on which their navigation was based was imported from <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionian Greeks</a>, who used it to become a seafaring nation of merchants and colonists during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_Greece" title="Archaic period in Greece">Archaic period in Greece</a>. Massalia was an Ionian colony. The first Ionian philosopher, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a>, was known for his ability to measure the distance of a ship at sea from a cliff by the very method Pytheas used to determine the latitude of Massalia, the trigonometric ratios.
</p><p>The astronomic model on which ancient Greek navigation was based, which is still in place today, was already extant in the time of Pytheas, the concept of the degrees only being missing. The model<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> divided the universe into a celestial and an earthly sphere pierced by the same poles. Each of the spheres were divided into zones (<i>zonai</i>) by circles (<i>kukloi</i>) in planes at right angles to the poles. The zones of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">celestial sphere</a> repeated on a larger scale those of the terrestrial sphere.
</p><p>The basis for division into zones was the two distinct paths of the heavenly bodies: that of the stars and that of the sun and moon. Astronomers know today that the Earth revolving around the sun is tilted on its axis, bringing each hemisphere now closer to the sun, now further away. The Greeks had the opposite model, that the stars and the sun rotated around the earth. The stars moved in fixed circles around the poles. The sun moved at an oblique angle to the circles, which obliquity brought it now to the north, now to the south. The circle of the sun was the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. It was the center of a band called the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a> on which various constellations were located.
</p><p>The shadow cast by a vertical rod at noon was the basis for defining zonation. The intersection of the northernmost or southernmost points of the ecliptic defined the axial circles passing through those points as the two tropics (<i>tropikoi kukloi</i>, "circles at the turning points") later named for the zodiacal constellations found there, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropic_of_cancer" title="Tropic of cancer">Cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn">Capricorn</a>. During noon of the <a href="/wiki/Summer_solstice" title="Summer solstice">summer solstice</a> (therinē tropē) rods there cast no shadow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The latitudes between the tropics were called the torrid zone (<i>diakekaumenē</i>, "burned up").
</p><p>Based on their experience of the Torrid Zone south of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, the Greek geographers judged it uninhabitable. Symmetry requires that there be an uninhabitable Frigid Zone (<i>katepsugmenē</i>, "frozen") to the north and reports from there since the time of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> seemed to confirm it. The edge of the Frigid Zone ought to be as far south from the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> in latitude as the Summer Tropic is from the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>. Strabo gives it as 24°, which may be based on a previous tangent of Pytheas, but he does not say. The Arctic Circle would then be at 66°, accurate to within a degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Seen from the equator the celestial North Pole (<i>boreios polos</i>) is a point on the horizon. As the observer moves northward the pole rises and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumpolar_stars" title="Circumpolar stars">circumpolar stars</a> appear, now unblocked by the Earth. At the Tropic of Cancer the radius of the circumpolar stars reaches 24°. The edge stands on the horizon. The <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> of <i>mikra arktos</i> (<a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor" title="Ursa Minor">Ursa Minor</a>, "little bear") was entirely contained within the circumpolar region. The latitude was therefore called the <i>arktikos kuklos</i>, "circle of the bear". The terrestrial Arctic Circle was regarded as fixed at this latitude. The celestial Arctic Circle was regarded as identical to the circumference of the circumpolar stars and therefore a variable.
</p><p>When the observer is on the terrestrial Arctic Circle and the radius of the circumpolar stars is 66° the celestial Arctic Circle is identical to the celestial <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> That is what Pytheas means when he says that Thule is located at the place where the Arctic Circle is identical to the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-5-8_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-5-8-28">[28]</a></sup> At that point, on the day of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summer_Solstice" title="Summer Solstice">Summer Solstice</a>, the vertical rod of the <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">gnōmōn</a> casts a shadow extending in theory to the horizon over 360° as the sun does not set. Under the pole the Arctic Circle is identical to the Equator and the sun never sets but rises and falls on the horizon. The shadow of the gnōmōn winds perpetually around it.
</p>
<h3><span id="Latitude_by_length_of_longest_day.2C_and_by_sun.27s_elevation_on_shortest_day"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitude_by_length_of_longest_day,_and_by_sun's_elevation_on_shortest_day">Latitude by length of longest day, and by sun's elevation on shortest day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Latitude by length of longest day, and by sun's elevation on shortest day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Strabo uses the astronomical cubit (<i>pēchus</i>, the length of the forearm from the elbow to the tip of the little finger) as a measure of the elevation of the sun. The term "cubit" in this context is obscure; it has nothing to do with distance along either a straight line or an arc, does not apply to celestial distances, and has nothing to do with the gnōmōn. <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a> borrowed this term from <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, where it meant 2°. They in turn took it from ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> so long ago that if the connection between cubits and degrees was known in either Babylonia or Ionia it did not survive. Strabo states degrees in either cubits or as a proportion of a <a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">great circle</a>. The Greeks also used the length of day at the summer solstice as a measure of latitude. It is stated in equinoctial hours (<i>hōrai isēmerinai</i>), one being 1/12 of the time between sunrise and sunset on an <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinox</a>.
</p><p>Based partly on data taken from Pytheas, Hipparchus correlated cubits of the sun's elevation at noon on the winter solstice, latitudes in hours of a day on the summer solstice, and distances between latitudes in stadia for some locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Pytheas had proved that Marseilles and Byzantium were on the same parallel (see above). Hipparchus, through Strabo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> adds that Byzantium and the mouth of the Borysthenes, today's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dnepr_river" title="Dnepr river">Dnepr river</a>, were on the same <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(geography)" title="Meridian (geography)">meridian</a> and were separated by 3700 stadia, 5.3° at Strabo's 700 stadia per a degree of <a href="/wiki/Meridian_arc" title="Meridian arc">meridian arc</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Circle_of_latitude" title="Circle of latitude">parallel</a> through the river-mouth also crossed the coast of "Celtica", the distance due north from Marseilles to Celtica was 3700 stadia, a baseline from which Pytheas seems to have calculated latitude and distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Strabo says that Ierne (Ireland) is under 5000 stadia (7.1°) north of this line. These figures place Celtica around the mouth of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loire_river" title="Loire river">Loire river</a>, an emporium for the trading of British tin. The part of Ireland referenced is the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>. Pytheas then would either have crossed the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Biscay" title="Bay of Biscay">Bay of Biscay</a> from the coast of Spain to the mouth of the Loire, or reached it along the coast, crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_channel" title="English channel">English channel</a> from the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Brest,_France" title="Brest, France">Brest, France</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, and traversed the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> to reach the <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> Islands. A statement of Eratosthenes attributed by Strabo to Pytheas, that the north of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> was an easier passage to Celtica than across the Ocean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> is somewhat ambiguous: apparently he knew or knew of both routes, but he does not say which he took.
</p><p>At noon on the <a href="/wiki/Winter_solstice" title="Winter solstice">winter solstice</a> the sun stands at 9 cubits and the longest day on the <a href="/wiki/Summer_solstice" title="Summer solstice">summer solstice</a> is 16 hours at the baseline through Celtica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> At 2500 stadia, approximately 283 miles, or 3.6°, north of Celtica, are a people Hipparchus called Celtic, but whom Strabo thinks are the British, a discrepancy he might not have noted if he had known that the British were also Celtic. The location is Cornwall. The sun stands at 6 cubits and the longest day is 17 hours. At 9100 stadia, approximately 1032 miles, north of Marseilles, 5400 or 7.7° north of Celtica, the elevation is 4 cubits and the longest day is 18 hours. This location is in the vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a>.
</p><p>Here Strabo launches another quibble. Hipparchus, relying on Pytheas, according to Strabo, places this area south of Britain, but he, Strabo, calculates that it is north of Ierne. Pytheas, however, rightly knows what is now <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> as part of Britain, land of the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, even though north of Ierne. North of southern Scotland the longest day is 19 hours. Strabo, based on theory alone, states that Ierne is so cold<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-5-8_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-5-8-28">[28]</a></sup> that any lands north of it must be uninhabited. In the hindsight given to moderns Pytheas, in relying on observation in the field, appears more scientific than Strabo, who discounted the findings of others merely because of their strangeness to him. The ultimate cause of his skepticism is simply that he did not believe Scandinavia could exist. This disbelief may also be the cause of alteration of Pytheas' data.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_tides">On the tides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: On the tides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pliny reports that "Pytheas of Massalia informs us, that in Britain the tide rises 80 cubits."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The passage does not give enough information to determine which cubit Pliny meant; however, any cubit gives the same general result. If he was reading an early source, the cubit may have been the <a href="/wiki/Cubit#Other_important_cubits" title="Cubit">Cyrenaic</a> cubit, an early Greek cubit, of 463.1 mm, in which case the distance was 37 metres (121 ft). This number far exceeds any modern known tides. The <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanography_Centre" title="National Oceanography Centre">National Oceanography Centre</a>, which records tides at tidal gauges placed in about 55 ports of the UK Tide Gauge Network on an ongoing basis, records the highest mean tidal change between 1987 and 2007 at <a href="/wiki/Avonmouth" title="Avonmouth">Avonmouth</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Severn_Estuary" title="Severn Estuary">Severn Estuary</a> of 6.955 m (22.82 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The highest predicted spring <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tide</a> between 2008 and 2026 at that location will be 14.64 m (48.0 ft) on 29 September 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Even allowing for geologic and climate change, Pytheas' 80 cubits far exceeds any known tides around Britain. One well-circulated but unevidenced answer to the paradox is that Pytheas is referring to a storm surge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tozerxxi_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tozerxxi-4">[4]</a></sup> Despite the modern arguments, the fact remains that Pytheas experienced tides that exceeded by far the usual tides in the Mediterranean, and particularly those at Massilia.
</p><p>Matching fragments of <a href="/wiki/Aetius_(philosopher)" title="Aetius (philosopher)">Aëtius</a> in pseudo-<a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stobaeus" title="Stobaeus">Stobaeus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> attribute the flood tides (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">πλήμμυραι</span> <i>plēmmurai</i>) to the "filling of the moon" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">πλήρωσις τῆς σελήνης</span> <i>plērōsis tēs sēlēnēs</i>) and the ebb tides (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἀμπώτιδες</span> <i>ampōtides</i>) to the "lessening" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">μείωσις</span> <i>meiōsis</i>). The words are too ambiguous to make an exact determination of Pytheas' meaning, whether diurnal or <a href="/wiki/Tide#Range_variation:_springs_and_neaps" title="Tide">spring and neap tides</a> are meant, or whether full and new moons or the half-cycles in which they occur. Different translators take different views.
</p><p>That daily tides should be caused by full moons and new moons is manifestly wrong, which would be a surprising view in a Greek astronomer and mathematician of the times. He could have meant that spring and neap tides were caused by new and full moons, which is partially correct in that spring tides occur at those times. A gravitational theory (objects fall to the center) existed at the time but Pytheas appears to have meant that the phases themselves were the causes (αἰτίαι <i>aitiai</i>). However imperfect or imperfectly related the viewpoint, Pytheas was the first to associate the tides to the <a href="/wiki/Moon#Tidal_effects" title="Moon">phases of the moon</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_influence">Literary influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pytheas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Literary influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pytheas was a central source of information on the North Sea and the subarctic regions of western Europe to later periods, and possibly the only source. The only ancient authors we know by name who certainly saw Pytheas' original text were <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">Dicaearchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(historian)" title="Timaeus (historian)">Timaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crates_of_Mallus" title="Crates of Mallus">Crates of Mallus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hipparchus" title="Hipparchus">Hipparchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artemidorus_Ephesius" title="Artemidorus Ephesius">Artemidorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Notably the list does not include <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>, though Strabo discusses him and Tacitus may likely have known about his work. Either of the two could have known him through other writers or have read his work in the original.
</p><p>Strabo, citing <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a>, accuses Pytheas of promulgating a fictitious journey he could never have funded, as he was a private individual (<i>idiōtēs</i>) and a poor man (<i>penēs</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboII-4-2_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-straboII-4-2-27">[27]</a></sup> Markham proposes a possible answer to the funding question:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> seeing that Pytheas was known as a professional geographer and that north Europe was as yet a question mark to Massalian merchants, he suggests that "the enterprise was a government expedition of which Pytheas was placed in command." In another suggestion the merchants of Marseilles sent him out to find northern markets. These theories are speculative but perhaps less so than Strabo's contention that Pytheas was a charlatan just because a professional geographer doubted him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Strabo does explain his reasons for doubting Pytheas' veracity. Citing numerous instances of Pytheas apparently being far off the mark on details concerning known regions, he says: "however, any man who has told such great falsehoods about the known regions would hardly, I imagine, be able to tell the truth about places that are not known to anybody."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboI-4-3_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-straboI-4-3-43">[43]</a></sup> As an example he mentions that Pytheas says <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> is several days' sail from Celtica when it is visible from Gaul across the channel. If Pytheas had visited the place he should have verified it personally.
</p><p>The objection although partially true is itself flawed. Strabo interjects his own view of the location of Celtica, that it was opposite to Britain, end to end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-straboI-4-3_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-straboI-4-3-43">[43]</a></sup> Pytheas, however, places it further south, around the mouth of the Loire (see above), from which it might justifiably be several days' sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The people across from Britain in Caesar's time are the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germani</a> in the north and the <a href="/wiki/Belgae" title="Belgae">Belgae</a> in the south. Still, some of the Celtic lands were on the channel and were visible from it, which Pytheas should have mentioned but Strabo implies he did not.
</p><p>Strabo's other objections are similarly flawed or else completely wrong. He simply did not believe the earth was inhabited north of Ierne. Pytheas however could not then answer for himself, or protect his own work from loss or alteration, so most of the questions concerning his voyage remain unresolved, to be worked over by every generation. To some he is a daring adventurer and discoverer;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> to others, a semi-legendary blunderer or prevaricator.
</p><p>The logical outcome of this tendency is the historical novel with Pytheas as the main character and the celebration of Pytheas in poetry beginning as far back as <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>. The process continues into modern times; for example, Pytheas is a key theme in <a href="/wiki/Charles_Olson" title="Charles Olson">Charles Olson</a>'s <i>Maximus Poems</i>.  Details of Pytheas' voyage also serve as the backdrop for Chapter I of <a href="/wiki/Poul_Anderson" title="Poul Anderson">Poul Anderson</a>'s science fiction novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boat_of_a_Million_Years" title="The Boat of a Million Years">The Boat of a Million Years</a></i>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nelson points out that this passage in Strabo contains "ambiguity": he could mean either one drink made from grain and honey, in which case it would have to be mead unless one classified it as a combination of mead and beer, or two drinks, mead and beer. Strabo uses the singular <i>pōma</i> for "beverage" but the neuter singular does not exclude a type of which there are two specifics. Some mead also is and was made with hops and is strained briefly through grain (see <a href="/wiki/Mead" title="Mead">mead</a>) The issue remains. See <cite class="citation book">Nelson, Max (2005). <i>The Barbarian's Beverage: A History of Beer in Ancient Europe</i>. Routledge. p. 64. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-415-31121-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-415-31121-7">0-415-31121-7</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Barbarian%27s+Beverage%3A+A+History+of+Beer+in+Ancient+Europe&amp;rft.pages=64&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=0-415-31121-7&amp;rft.aulast=Nelson&amp;rft.aufirst=Max&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APytheas"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most students of Pytheas presume that his differences from modern calculations represent error due to primitive instrumentation. Rawlins assumes the opposite, that Pytheas observed the sun correctly, but his observatory was a few miles south of west-facing Marseilles. Working backward from the discrepancy, he arrives at Maire Island or Cape Croisette, which Pytheas would have selected for better viewing over the south horizon. To date there is no archaeological or other evidence to support the presence of such an observatory; however, the deficit of antiquities does not prove non-existence. <cite class="citation journal">Rawlins, Dennis (December 2009). <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/vols/wg0.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Pytheas' Solstice Observation Locates Him"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>DIO &amp; the Journal for Hysterical Astronomy</i>. <b>16</b>: 11–17.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=DIO+%26+the+Journal+for+Hysterical+Astronomy&amp;rft.atitle=Pytheas%27+Solstice+Observation+Locates+Him&amp;rft.volume=16&amp;rft.pages=11-17&amp;rft.date=2009-12&amp;rft.aulast=Rawlins&amp;rft.aufirst=Dennis&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.dioi.org%2Fvols%2Fwg0.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APytheas"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Bowditch" title="Nathaniel Bowditch">Bowditch, Nathaniel</a> (2002). <a class="external text" href="http://msi.nga.mil/MSISiteContent/StaticFiles/NAV_PUBS/APN/Chapt-15.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>The American Practical Navigator: an Epitome of Navigation</i></a> <span class="cs1-format">(pdf)</span> (Bicentennial ed.). National Geospatial-Intelligence Agency. p. 243<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">7 June</span> 2012</span>. <q>That is, the altitude of the elevated pole is equal to the declination of the zenith, which is equal to the latitude</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+American+Practical+Navigator%3A+an+Epitome+of+Navigation&amp;rft.pages=243&amp;rft.edition=Bicentennial&amp;rft.pub=National+Geospatial-Intelligence+Agency&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Bowditch&amp;rft.aufirst=Nathaniel&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmsi.nga.mil%2FMSISiteContent%2FStaticFiles%2FNAV_PUBS%2FAPN%2FChapt-15.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APytheas"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
